<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg/220px-Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg/330px-Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg/440px-Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lancaster_County_Amish_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a> family riding in a traditional Amish buggy in <a href="/wiki/Lancaster_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster County, Pennsylvania">Lancaster County, Pennsylvania</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Of all the colonies, only <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a> had religious freedom as secure as in Pennsylvania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, writing of <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a> in 1733, observed: "The new sovereign also enacted several wise and wholesome laws for his colony, which have remained invariably the same to this day. The chief is, to ill–treat no person on account of religion, and to consider as brethren all those who believe in one God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> One result of this uncommon freedom was a wide religious <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">diversity</a>, which continues to the present.</p>
<p>Pennsylvania's population in 2010 was 12,702,379. Of these, 6,838,440 (53.8%) were estimated to belong to some sort of organized religion. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_of_religion_data_archives" title="Association of religion data archives">Association of religion data archives</a> (ARDA) at <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a>, the largest religions in Pennsylvania by adherents are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> with 3,503,028 adherents, the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> with 591,734 members, and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_in_America" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church in America">Evangelical Lutheran Church in America</a> with 501,974 members.</p>
<p>The third-largest single denomination is the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(USA)" title="Presbyterian Church (USA)">Presbyterian Church (USA)</a> with 250,000 members and 1,011 congregations. Pennsylvania, especially western part and Pittsburgh area has one of the highest percentage of Presbyterians in the nation. The Presbyterian Church in America has also significant, 112 congregations and 23,000 adherents, the EPC has around 50 congregations, as well as ECO. The next largest <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> denomination, the <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Christ" title="United Church of Christ">United Church of Christ</a>, has 180,000 members and 627 congregations. <a href="/wiki/American_Baptist_Churches_USA" title="American Baptist Churches USA">American Baptist Churches USA</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Baptist_Convention" title="Northern Baptist Convention">Northern Baptist Convention</a>) is based in <a href="/wiki/King_of_Prussia,_Pennsylvania" title="King of Prussia, Pennsylvania">King of Prussia, Pennsylvania</a>.</p>
<p>Pennsylvania was the center state of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Reformed" title="German Reformed">German Reformed</a> denomination since the 1700s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem,_Pennsylvania" title="Bethlehem, Pennsylvania">Bethlehem, Pennsylvania</a>, is one of the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravian Church</a> in America. Pennsylvania also has a very large <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a> population, second only to <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> among the states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In the year 2000 there was a total <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">Amish</a> population of 47,860 in Pennsylvania and a further 146,416 <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonites</a> and 91,200 <a href="/wiki/Schwarzenau_Brethren" title="Schwarzenau Brethren">Brethren</a>. The total Anabapist population including <a href="/wiki/Bruderhof_Communities" title="Bruderhof Communities">Bruderhof</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> was 232,631, about 2 percent of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> While Pennsylvania owes its existence to <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a>, and much of the historic character of the Commonwealth is ideologically rooted in the teachings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" title="Religious Society of Friends">Religious Society of Friends</a> (as they are officially known), practicing Quakers are a small minority of about 10,000 adherents in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>The religious affiliations of the people of Pennsylvania:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pew_Religion_and_Politics_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Religion_and_Politics-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Christianity – 80%
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> – 50%
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainline_(Protestant)" title="Mainline (Protestant)">Mainline Protestant</a> – 25%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical Protestant</a> – 18%</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Black_church" title="Black church">Black Protestant</a> – 7%</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a> – 1%</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> – 29%</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" title="Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a> – 1%</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-religious" title="Non-religious">Non-religious</a>/<a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">Unaffiliated</a> – 13%</li>
<li>Judaism – 2%</li>
<li>Hinduism – 1%</li>
<li>Other religions – 1%</li>
<li>Don't know/Refused to say – 1%</li>
</ul>
<p>According to a 2012 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Gallup_Organization" title="The Gallup Organization">Gallup</a> poll, 40% of Pennsylvanians are very religious, 28% are moderately religious, and 32% are non-religious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Pennsylvania_counties_by_per_capita_income" title="List of Pennsylvania counties by per capita income">List of Pennsylvania counties by per capita income</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Pennsylvania_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="List of Pennsylvania locations by per capita income">List of Pennsylvania locations by per capita income</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carrie_Furnace030.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Carrie_Furnace030.jpg/250px-Carrie_Furnace030.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Carrie_Furnace030.jpg/375px-Carrie_Furnace030.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Carrie_Furnace030.jpg/500px-Carrie_Furnace030.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bethlehem_Steel.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="1500" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bethlehem_Steel.jpg/250px-Bethlehem_Steel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bethlehem_Steel.jpg/375px-Bethlehem_Steel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bethlehem_Steel.jpg/500px-Bethlehem_Steel.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:top/bottom;background-color:">Pennsylvania was home of two of the largest steel producers in the world; <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>-based <a href="/wiki/U.S._Steel" title="U.S. Steel">U.S. Steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem,_Pennsylvania" title="Bethlehem, Pennsylvania">Bethlehem</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem_Steel" title="Bethlehem Steel">Bethlehem Steel</a>. The former blast furnace sites have either been destroyed, <a href="/wiki/List_of_preserved_historic_blast_furnaces" title="List of preserved historic blast furnaces">preserved</a>, or in the case of Bethlehem, became a new multi-million dollar <a href="/wiki/Sands_Casino_Resort_Bethlehem" title="Sands Casino Resort Bethlehem">Sands Casino Resort</a> in 2009.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG/300px-Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG/450px-Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG/600px-Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sorry_We%27re_Open_-_sign._Philadelphia.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Sorry We're Open" sign, <a href="/wiki/West_Chester,_Pennsylvania" title="West Chester, Pennsylvania">West Chester</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pennsylvania's 2013 total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_state_product" title="Gross state product">gross state product</a> (GSP) of $644 billion ranks the state 6th in the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stategdp_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-stategdp-93">[93]</a></sup> If Pennsylvania were an independent country, its economy would rank as the 18th-largest in the world. On a per-capita basis, Pennsylvania's per-capita GSP of $47,274 (in chained 2009 dollars) ranks 26th among the 50 states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stategdp_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-stategdp-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="882" data-file-width="1122" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png/220px-Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png/330px-Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png/440px-Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geo_Map_of_Income_by_Location_in_Pennsylvania.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Geo map of average income by location in Pennsylvania. Data shown is from the 2014 American Community Survey 5-year estimate.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> in the southeast corner, <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> in the southwest corner, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erie" title="Erie">Erie</a> in the northwest corner, <a href="/wiki/Wyoming_Valley" title="Wyoming Valley">Scranton-Wilkes-Barre</a> in the northeast corner, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allentown-Bethlehem-Easton" title="Allentown-Bethlehem-Easton">Allentown-Bethlehem-Easton</a> in the east central region are urban manufacturing centers. Much of the Commonwealth is rural; this dichotomy affects state politics as well as the state economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Philadelphia is home to six <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> companies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F500_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-F500-95">[95]</a></sup> with more located in suburbs like <a href="/wiki/King_of_Prussia,_Pennsylvania" title="King of Prussia, Pennsylvania">King of Prussia</a>; it is a leader in the financial<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and insurance industry.</p>
<p>Pittsburgh is home to eight <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> companies, including <a href="/wiki/U.S._Steel" title="U.S. Steel">U.S. Steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/PPG_Industries" title="PPG Industries">PPG Industries</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.J._Heinz" title="H.J. Heinz">H.J. Heinz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F500_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-F500-95">[95]</a></sup> In all, Pennsylvania is home to fifty Fortune 500 companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-F500_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-F500-95">[95]</a></sup> Erie is also home to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GE_Transportation_Systems" title="GE Transportation Systems">GE Transportation Systems</a>, which is the largest producer of train locomotives in the United States.</p>
<p>As in the US as a whole and in most states, the largest private employer in the Commonwealth is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wal-Mart" title="Wal-Mart">Wal-Mart</a>, followed by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Pennsylvania is also home to the oldest investor-owned utility company in the U.S.A, <a href="/wiki/The_York_Water_Company" title="The York Water Company">The York Water Company</a>.</p>
<p>As of June 2014, the state's unemployment rate is 5.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banking">Banking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Banking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first nationally chartered bank in the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_North_America" title="Bank of North America">Bank of North America</a>, was founded in 1781 in Philadelphia. After a series of mergers, the Bank of North America is part of <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo" title="Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo</a>, which uses national charter 1.</p>
<p>Pennsylvania is also the home to the first nationally chartered bank under the 1863 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Banking_Act" title="National Banking Act">National Banking Act</a>. That year, the Pittsburgh Savings &amp; Trust Company received a national charter and renamed itself the First National Bank of Pittsburgh as part of the National Banking Act. That bank is still in existence today as <a href="/wiki/PNC_Financial_Services" title="PNC Financial Services">PNC Financial Services</a> and remains based in Pittsburgh. PNC is the state's largest bank, and the sixth-largest in the United States.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Pennsylvania" title="Agriculture in Pennsylvania">Agriculture in Pennsylvania</a></div>
<p><b>Pennsylvania ranks 19th overall in agricultural production</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ag_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-ag-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The 1st is <a href="/wiki/Fungiculture" title="Fungiculture">mushroom</a> production,</li>
<li>The 2nd is apples,</li>
<li>The 3rd is <a href="/wiki/Christmas_tree" title="Christmas tree">Christmas trees</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">layer chickens</a>,</li>
<li>The 4th is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nursery_(horticulture)" title="Nursery (horticulture)">nursery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sod" title="Sod">sod</a>, milk, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> for <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a>, grapes grown (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grape_juice" title="Grape juice">juice grapes</a>), and horses production.</li>
</ul>
<p>It also ranks 8th in the nation in <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">Winemaking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAwine_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAwine-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Department_of_Agriculture" title="Pennsylvania Department of Agriculture">Pennsylvania Department of Agriculture</a> worked with private companies to establish "PA Preferred" as a way to brand agricultural products grown or made in the state to support and promote Pennsylvania products and locally-grown food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The financial impact of agriculture in Pennsylvania<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> includes employment of more than 66,800 people are employed by the food <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> industry; and over $1.7 billion in food product <a href="/wiki/Export" title="Export">export</a> (in 2011).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gambling">Gambling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gambling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gambling_in_Pennsylvania" title="Gambling in Pennsylvania">Gambling in Pennsylvania</a></div>
<p>Casino gambling was legalized in Pennsylvania in 2004. Currently, there are nine casinos across the state with three under construction or in planning. Only horse racing, slot machines and electronic table games were legal in Pennsylvania, although a bill to legalize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Table_games" title="Table games">table games</a> was being negotiated in the fall of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Table games such as poker, roulette, blackjack and craps were finally approved by the state legislature in January 2010, being signed into law by the Governor on January 7. Sports betting is illegal.</p>
<p>Former Governor <a href="/wiki/Ed_Rendell" title="Ed Rendell">Ed Rendell</a> had considered legalizing video poker machines in bars and private clubs in 2009, since an estimated 17,000 operate illegally across the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Under this plan, any establishment with a liquor license would be allowed up to 5 machines. All machines would be connected to the state's computer system, like commercial casinos. The state would impose a 50% tax on net gambling revenues, after winning players have been paid, with the remaining 50% going to the establishment owners.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Film_Production_Tax_Credit" title="Pennsylvania Film Production Tax Credit">Pennsylvania Film Production Tax Credit</a> began in 2004 and stimulated the development of a film industry in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bizjournal_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-bizjournal-106">[106]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Pennsylvania" title="Government of Pennsylvania">Government of Pennsylvania</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_(U.S._state)" title="Commonwealth (U.S. state)">Commonwealth (U.S. state)</a></div>
<p>Pennsylvania has had five <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Constitution" title="Pennsylvania Constitution">constitutions</a> during its statehood:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jenkinslaw_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-jenkinslaw-109">[109]</a></sup> 1776, 1790, 1838, 1874, and <a class="external text" href="http://sites.state.pa.us/PA_Constitution.html" rel="nofollow">1968</a>. Before that the province of Pennsylvania was governed for a century by a <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_Government_of_Pennsylvania" title="Frame of Government of Pennsylvania">Frame of Government</a>, of which there were four versions: 1682, 1683, 1696, and 1701.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jenkinslaw_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-jenkinslaw-109">[109]</a></sup> The capital of Pennsylvania is <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a>. The legislature meets in the State Capitol there.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_Pennsylvania" title="Political party strength in Pennsylvania">Political party strength in Pennsylvania</a></div>
<p>For most of the second half of the 20th century and into the 21st century, Pennsylvania has been a powerful <a href="/wiki/Swing_state" title="Swing state">swing state</a>. It only supported the losing candidate in a presidential election twice from 1932 to 1988. Since 1992, Pennsylvania has been trending Democratic in Presidential elections, voting for Bill Clinton twice by large margins, and slightly closer in 2000 for Al Gore. In the 2004 Presidential Election, Senator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_F._Kerry" title="John F. Kerry">John F. Kerry</a> beat President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in Pennsylvania 2,938,095 (50.92%) to 2,793,847 (48.42%). In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2008" title="United States presidential election, 2008">2008 Presidential Election</a>, Democrat <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> defeated Republican <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a> in Pennsylvania, 3,184,778 (54%) to 2,584,088 (44%). Most recently, in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 Presidential Election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> became the first Republican candidate to win the state since 1988, winning the state 48.6% to 47.8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The state holds 20 <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral</a> votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAFacts_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-PAFacts-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent national elections, Pennsylvania has leaned <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a>. The state voted for the Democratic ticket for president in every election between 1992 and 2012. During the 2008 election campaign a recruitment drive saw registered Democrats outnumber registered Republicans by 1.2 million. However, Pennsylvania has a history of electing Republican senators. From 2009 to 2011, the state was represented by two Democratic senators for the first time since 1947. In 2010, Republicans recaptured a U.S. Senate seat as well as a majority of the state's congressional seats, control of both chambers of the state legislature and the governor's mansion. Democrats won back the governor's mansion 4 years later in the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_gubernatorial_election,_2014" title="Pennsylvania gubernatorial election, 2014">2014 election</a>. It was the first time since a governor became eligible to succeed himself that an incumbent governor had been defeated for reelection.</p>
<p>Historically, Democratic strength was concentrated in Philadelphia in the southeast, the Pittsburgh and <a href="/wiki/Johnstown,_Pennsylvania" title="Johnstown, Pennsylvania">Johnstown</a> areas in the southwest, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scranton" title="Scranton">Scranton</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilkes-Barre" title="Wilkes-Barre">Wilkes-Barre</a> in the northeast. Republican strength was concentrated in the Philadelphia suburbs, as well as the more rural areas in the central, northeastern, and western portions. The latter counties have long been among the most conservative areas in the nation. Since 1992, however, the Philadelphia suburbs have swung Democratic; the brand of Republicanism there was traditionally a moderate one. The Pittsburgh suburbs, historically a Democratic stronghold, have swung more Republican since the turn of the millennium.</p>
<p>Democratic political consultant <a href="/wiki/James_Carville" title="James Carville">James Carville</a> once pejoratively described Pennsylvania as "Philadelphia in the east, Pittsburgh in the west and Alabama in the middle". Political analysts and editorials refer to central Pennsylvania as the "T" in statewide elections. Philadelphia, Pittsburgh, and Scranton/Wilkes-Barre generally vote for Democratic candidates, while the majority of the counties in the central part of the state vote Republican. As a result, maps showing the results of statewide elections invariably form a "T" shape.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor">Governor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Governor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Pennsylvania" title="List of Governors of Pennsylvania">List of Governors of Pennsylvania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1952" data-file-width="1409" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG/220px-PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG/330px-PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG/440px-PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PAState_Capitol_Back_a_Bit.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_Capitol" title="Pennsylvania State Capitol">Pennsylvania State Capitol</a> in <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The current Governor is <a href="/wiki/Tom_Wolf_(politician)" title="Tom Wolf (politician)">Tom Wolf</a>. The other elected officials composing the executive branch are the <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Pennsylvania" title="Lieutenant Governor of Pennsylvania">Lieutenant Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Stack_III" title="Michael J. Stack III">Michael Stack</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Attorney_General" title="Pennsylvania Attorney General">Attorney General</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joshua_Shapiro" title="Joshua Shapiro">Joshua Shapiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Auditor_General" title="Pennsylvania Auditor General">Auditor General</a> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_DePasquale" title="Eugene DePasquale">Eugene DePasquale</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Treasurer" title="Pennsylvania Treasurer">Pennsylvania Treasurer</a> <a href="/wiki/Joe_Torsella" title="Joe Torsella">Joe Torsella</a>. Governor and Lieutenant Governor run as a ticket in the general election and are up for reelection in 2018. Pennsylvania will hold elections for Attorney General, Auditor General and Treasurer in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Pennsylvania_state_agencies" title="List of Pennsylvania state agencies">List of Pennsylvania state agencies</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Assembly">General Assembly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: General Assembly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_General_Assembly" title="Pennsylvania General Assembly">Pennsylvania General Assembly</a></div>
<p>Pennsylvania has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicameral_legislature" title="Bicameral legislature">bicameral legislature</a> set up by Commonwealth's constitution in 1790. The original Frame of Government of William Penn had a unicameral legislature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_General_Assembly" title="Pennsylvania General Assembly">General Assembly</a> includes 50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Senate" title="Pennsylvania Senate">Senators</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and 203 <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_House_of_Representatives" title="Pennsylvania House of Representatives">Representatives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Scarnati_III" title="Joseph B. Scarnati III">Joe Scarnati</a> is currently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_Pro_Tempore" title="President Pro Tempore">President Pro Tempore</a> of the State Senate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominic_Pileggi" title="Dominic Pileggi">Dominic Pileggi</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Majority_Leader" title="Majority Leader">Majority Leader</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jay_Costa" title="Jay Costa">Jay Costa</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minority_Leader" title="Minority Leader">Minority Leader</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_H._Smith_(politician)" title="Samuel H. Smith (politician)">Sam Smith</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_Pennsylvania_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the Pennsylvania House of Representatives">Speaker</a> of the House of Representatives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Mike_Turzai" title="Mike Turzai">Mike Turzai</a> as Majority Leader<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Dermody" title="Frank Dermody">Frank Dermody</a> as Minority Leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> As of the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_House_of_Representatives_elections,_2012" title="Pennsylvania House of Representatives elections, 2012">2012 elections</a>, the Republicans hold the majority in the State House and Senate.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Judiciary">Judiciary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Judiciary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unified_Judicial_System_of_Pennsylvania" title="Unified Judicial System of Pennsylvania">Unified Judicial System of Pennsylvania</a></div>
<p>Pennsylvania is divided into 60 judicial districts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courts_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-courts-121">[121]</a></sup> most of which (except <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia County, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia</a>) have magisterial district judges (formerly called district justices and justices of the peace), who preside mainly over preliminary hearings in felony and misdemeanor offenses, all minor (summary) criminal offenses, and small civil claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courts_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-courts-121">[121]</a></sup> Most criminal and civil cases originate in the Courts of Common Pleas, which also serve as <a href="/wiki/Appellate_court" title="Appellate court">appellate courts</a> to the district judges and for local agency decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courts_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-courts-121">[121]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Superior_Court_of_Pennsylvania" title="Superior Court of Pennsylvania">Superior Court</a> hears all appeals from the Courts of Common Pleas not expressly designated to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Court_of_Pennsylvania" title="Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania">Commonwealth Court</a> or Supreme Court. It also has <a href="/wiki/Original_jurisdiction" title="Original jurisdiction">original jurisdiction</a> to review <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(law)" title="Warrant (law)">warrants</a> for <a href="/wiki/Telephone_tapping" title="Telephone tapping">wiretap</a> surveillance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courts_121-3"><a href="#cite_note-courts-121">[121]</a></sup> The Commonwealth Court is limited to appeals from final orders of certain state agencies and certain designated cases from the Courts of Common Pleas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courts_121-4"><a href="#cite_note-courts-121">[121]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Pennsylvania" title="Supreme Court of Pennsylvania">Supreme Court of Pennsylvania</a> is the final appellate court. All judges in Pennsylvania are elected; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chief_justice" title="Chief justice">chief justice</a> is determined by seniority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-courts_121-5"><a href="#cite_note-courts-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pennsylvania has the 10th-highest tax burden in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Residents pay a total of $83.7 billion in state and local taxes with a per capita average of $6,640 annually. Residents share 76% of the total tax burden. Many state politicians have tried to increase the share of taxes paid by out of state sources. Suggested revenue sources include taxing natural gas drilling as Pennsylvania is the only state without such a tax on gas drilling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Additional revenue prospects include trying to place tolls on interstate highways; specifically <a href="/wiki/Interstate_80_in_Pennsylvania" title="Interstate 80 in Pennsylvania">Interstate 80</a> which is used heavily by out of state commuters with high maintenance costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sales_tax" title="Sales tax">Sales tax</a> provides 39% of Commonwealth's revenue; <a href="/wiki/State_income_tax" title="State income tax">personal income tax</a> 34%; motor vehicle taxes about 12%, and taxes on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cigarette_tax#Taxation" title="Cigarette tax">cigarettes</a> and alcohol beverage 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Personal income tax is a flat 3.07%. An individual's taxable income is based on the following eight types of income: compensation (salary); interest; dividends; net profits from the operation of a business, profession or farm; net gains or income from the dispositions of property; net gains or income from rents, royalties, patents and copyrights; income derived through estates or trusts; and gambling and <a href="/wiki/Lottery" title="Lottery">lottery</a> winnings (other than <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Lottery" title="Pennsylvania Lottery">Pennsylvania Lottery</a> winnings).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Counties, municipalities, and <a href="/wiki/School_district" title="School district">school districts</a> levy taxes on real estate. In addition, some local bodies assess a <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">wage tax</a> on personal income. Generally, the total wage tax rate is capped at 1% of income but some municipalities with <a href="/wiki/Home_rule" title="Home rule">home rule</a> charters may charge more than 1%. Thirty-two of the Commonwealth's sixty-seven counties levy a <a href="/wiki/Property_tax" title="Property tax">personal property tax</a> on stocks, bonds, and similar holdings.</p>
<p>With the exception of the city of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, Pennsylvania municipalities and school districts are allowed to enact a local earned income tax within the purview of Act 32. Residents of these municipalities and school districts are required to file a local income tax return in addition to federal and state returns. This local return is filed with the local income tax collector, a private collection agency (e.g. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Berkheimer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Berkheimer (page does not exist)">Berkheimer</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Keystone_Collections&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Keystone Collections (page does not exist)">Keystone Collections</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jordan_Tax_Service&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jordan Tax Service (page does not exist)">Jordan Tax Service</a>) appointed by a particular county to collect the local earned income and local services tax (the latter a flat fee deducted from salaried employees working within a particular municipality or school district).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The City of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> has its own local income taxation system. Philadelphia-based employers are required to withhold the Philadelphia wage tax from the salaries of their employees. Residents of Philadelphia working for an employer are not required to file a local return as long as their Philadelphia wage tax is fully withheld by their employer. If their employer does not withhold the Philadelphia wage tax, residents are required to register with the Revenue Department and file an Earnings Tax return. Residents of Philadelphia with self-employment income are required to file a Net Profits tax (NPT) return, while those with business income from Philadelphia sources are required to obtain a Commercial Activity License (CAL) and pay the Business Income and Receipts tax (BIRT) and the NPT. Residents with unearned income (except for interest from checking and savings accounts) are required to file and pay the School Income tax (SIT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>The complexity of Pennsylvania local tax filing system has been criticized by experts, who note that the outsourcing collections to private entities is akin to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax_farming" title="Tax farming">tax farming</a> and that many new residents are caught off guard and end up facing "failure to file" penalties even if they did not have any tax due. Attempts to transfer local income tax collections to the state level (i.e. by having a separate local section on the state income tax return, currently the method used to collect local income taxes in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>) have been unsuccessful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_Representation">Federal Representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Federal Representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pennsylvania's two <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">U.S. Senators</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Casey,_Jr." title="Bob Casey, Jr.">Bob Casey, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Toomey" title="Pat Toomey">Pat Toomey</a>.</p>
<p>Pennsylvania's current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Representatives" title="U.S. Representatives">U.S. Representatives</a> are <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brady" title="Bob Brady">Bob Brady</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_1st_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 1st congressional district">1st</a>), <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Evans_(politician)" title="Dwight Evans (politician)">Dwight Evans</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_2nd_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 2nd congressional district">2nd</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mike_Kelly_(Pennsylvania)" title="Mike Kelly (Pennsylvania)">Mike Kelly</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_3rd_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 3rd congressional district">3rd</a>), <a href="/wiki/Scott_Perry_(politician)" title="Scott Perry (politician)">Scott Perry</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_4th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 4th congressional district">4th</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glenn_%22G.T.%22_Thompson" title='Glenn "G.T." Thompson'>Glenn "G.T." Thompson</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_5th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 5th congressional district">5th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Costello" title="Ryan Costello">Ryan Costello</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_6th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 6th congressional district">6th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Pat_Meehan" title="Pat Meehan">Pat Meehan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_7th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 7th congressional district">7th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Brian_Fitzpatrick_(American_politician)" title="Brian Fitzpatrick (American politician)">Brian Fitzpatrick</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_8th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 8th congressional district">8th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bill_Shuster" title="Bill Shuster">Bill Shuster</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_9th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 9th congressional district">9th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Tom_Marino" title="Tom Marino">Tom Marino</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_10th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 10th congressional district">10th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lou_Barletta" title="Lou Barletta">Lou Barletta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_11th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 11th congressional district">11th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Keith_Rothfus" title="Keith Rothfus">Keith Rothfus</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_12th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 12th congressional district">12th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Boyle" title="Brendan Boyle">Brendan Boyle</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_13th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 13th congressional district">13th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Michael_F._Doyle" title="Michael F. Doyle">Mike Doyle</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_14th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 14th congressional district">14th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Dent" title="Charlie Dent">Charlie Dent</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_15th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 15th congressional district">15th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Smucker" title="Lloyd Smucker">Lloyd Smucker</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_16th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 16th congressional district">16th</a>), <a href="/wiki/Matt_Cartwright" title="Matt Cartwright">Matt Cartwright</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_17th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 17th congressional district">17th</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tim_Murphy_(congressman)" title="Tim Murphy (congressman)">Tim Murphy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania%27s_18th_congressional_district" title="Pennsylvania's 18th congressional district">18th</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_congressional_districts#Pennsylvania" title="List of United States congressional districts"><i>See map of congressional districts</i></a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Municipalities">Municipalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Municipalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of counties in Pennsylvania">List of counties in Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of cities in Pennsylvania">List of cities in Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_towns_and_boroughs_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of towns and boroughs in Pennsylvania">List of towns and boroughs in Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_townships_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of townships in Pennsylvania">List of townships in Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_county_seats_in_Pennsylvania_(by_population)" title="List of county seats in Pennsylvania (by population)">List of county seats in Pennsylvania (by population)</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_census-designated_places_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of census-designated places in Pennsylvania">List of census-designated places in Pennsylvania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_populated_places_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of populated places in Pennsylvania">List of populated places in Pennsylvania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allentown.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Allentown.jpg/275px-Allentown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Allentown.jpg/413px-Allentown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Allentown.jpg/550px-Allentown.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allentown.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Allentown,_Pennsylvania" title="Allentown, Pennsylvania">Allentown, Pennsylvania</a>, 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pennsylvania is divided into 67 <a href="/wiki/County_(United_States)" title="County (United States)">counties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-3_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-3-134">[134]</a></sup> Counties are further subdivided into municipalities that are either incorporated as cities, <a href="/wiki/Borough_(Pennsylvania)" title="Borough (Pennsylvania)">boroughs</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Township_(Pennsylvania)" title="Township (Pennsylvania)">townships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-5_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-5-135">[135]</a></sup> One county, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia County, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia County</a>, is coterminous with the city of Philadelphia after it was <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Consolidation,_1854" title="Act of Consolidation, 1854">consolidated in 1854</a>. The most populous county in Pennsylvania is Philadelphia, while the least populous is <a href="/wiki/Cameron_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Cameron County, Pennsylvania">Cameron</a> (5,085).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pasdc.hbg.psu.edu_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-pasdc.hbg.psu.edu-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are a total of 56 cities in Pennsylvania, which are classified, by population, as either first-, second-, or third-class cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-3_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-3-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Philadelphia, Pennsylvania's largest city, has a population of 1,526,006 and is the state's only first-class city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-5_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-5-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a> (305,704) and <a href="/wiki/Scranton,_Pennsylvania" title="Scranton, Pennsylvania">Scranton</a> (76,089) are second-class and second-class 'A' cities, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-5_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-5-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rest of the cities, like the third and fourth-largest—<a href="/wiki/Allentown,_Pennsylvania" title="Allentown, Pennsylvania">Allentown</a> (118,032) and <a href="/wiki/Erie,_Pennsylvania" title="Erie, Pennsylvania">Erie</a> (101,786)—to the smallest—<a href="/wiki/Parker,_Pennsylvania" title="Parker, Pennsylvania">Parker</a> with a population of only 840—are third-class cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> First- and second-class cities are governed by a "strong mayor" form of <a href="/wiki/Mayor%E2%80%93council_government" title="Mayor–council government">mayor–council government</a>, whereas third-class cities are governed by either a "weak mayor" form of government or a <a href="/wiki/Council%E2%80%93manager_government" title="Council–manager government">council–manager government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-5_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-5-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boroughs are generally smaller than cities, with most Pennsylvania cities having been incorporated as a borough before being incorporated as a city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-5_135-4"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-5-135">[135]</a></sup> There are 958 boroughs in Pennsylvania, all of which governed by the "weak mayor" form of mayor–council government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-3_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-3-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-5_135-5"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-5-135">[135]</a></sup> The largest borough in Pennsylvania is <a href="/wiki/State_College,_Pennsylvania" title="State College, Pennsylvania">State College</a> (42,034) and the smallest is <a href="/wiki/Centralia,_Pennsylvania" title="Centralia, Pennsylvania">Centralia</a> (10).</p>
<p>Townships are the third type of municipality in Pennsylvania and are classified as either first-class or second-class townships. There are 1,454 second-class townships and 93 first-class townships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-6_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-6-138">[138]</a></sup> Second-class township can become first-class townships if it has a population density greater than 300 inhabitants per square mile (120/km<sup>2</sup>) and a <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a> is passed supporting the change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA_Manual_6-6_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA_Manual_6-6-138">[138]</a></sup> Pennsylvania's largest township is <a href="/wiki/Upper_Darby_Township,_Delaware_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Upper Darby Township, Delaware County, Pennsylvania">Upper Darby Township</a> (82,795), and the smallest is <a href="/wiki/East_Keating_Township,_Clinton_County,_Pennsylvania" title="East Keating Township, Clinton County, Pennsylvania">East Keating Township</a> (11).</p>
<p>There is one exception to the types of municipalities in Pennsylvania: <a href="/wiki/Bloomsburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Bloomsburg, Pennsylvania">Bloomsburg</a> was incorporated as a town in 1870 and is, officially, the only town in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> In 1975, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McCandless_Township,_Allegheny_County,_Pennsylvania" title="McCandless Township, Allegheny County, Pennsylvania">McCandless Township</a> adopted a home-rule charter under the name of "Town of McCandless", but is, legally, still a first-class township.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>The total of 56 cities, 958 boroughs, 93 first-class townships, 1,454 second-class townships, and one town (Bloomsburg) is 2,562 municipalities.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_municipalities_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of municipalities in Pennsylvania">List of municipalities in Pennsylvania</a></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pennsylvania has a mixed health record, and is ranked as the 29th-overall-healthiest state according to the 2013 United Health Foundation's Health Rankings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahr_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-ahr-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Pennsylvania" title="Education in Pennsylvania">Education in Pennsylvania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Penn_campus_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="1024" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Penn_campus_2.jpg/220px-Penn_campus_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Penn_campus_2.jpg/330px-Penn_campus_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Penn_campus_2.jpg/440px-Penn_campus_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Penn_campus_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a> quadrangle in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pennsylvania has 500 public school districts, thousands of private schools, publicly funded colleges and universities, and over 100 private institutions of higher education.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_and_secondary_education">Primary and secondary education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Primary and secondary education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In general, under state law, school attendance in Pennsylvania is mandatory for a child from the age of 8 until the age of 17, or until graduation from an accredited high school, whichever is earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> As of 2005, 83.8% of Pennsylvania residents age 18 to 24 have completed high school. Among residents age 25 and over, 86.7% have graduated from high school.</p>
<p>The following are the four-year graduation rates for students completing high school in 2015:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Cohort</th>
<th>All Students</th>
<th>Male</th>
<th>Female</th>
<th>White</th>
<th>Hispanic</th>
<th>Black</th>
<th>Asian</th>
<th>Special Education</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td> % graduating</td>
<td>84.8</td>
<td>82.2</td>
<td>87.3</td>
<td>89.3</td>
<td>69.5</td>
<td>71.8</td>
<td>90.7</td>
<td>71.5</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Additionally, 25.7% have gone on to obtain a bachelor's degree or higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> State students consistently do well in standardized testing. In 2007, Pennsylvania ranked 14th in mathematics, 12th in reading, and 10th in writing for 8th grade students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1988, the Pennsylvania General Assembly passed Act 169, which allows parents or guardians to homeschool their children as an option for compulsory school attendance. This law specifies the requirements and responsibilities of the parents and the school district where the family lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education">Higher education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Higher education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of colleges and universities in Pennsylvania">List of colleges and universities in Pennsylvania</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_System_of_Higher_Education" title="Pennsylvania State System of Higher Education">Pennsylvania State System of Higher Education</a> (PASSHE) is the <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public university</a> system of the Commonwealth, with 14 state-owned schools. The <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_System_of_Higher_Education" title="Commonwealth System of Higher Education">Commonwealth System of Higher Education</a> is an organizing body of the 4 state-related schools in Pennsylvania, these schools are independent institutions that receive some state funding. There are also 15 publicly funded two-year <a href="/wiki/Community_colleges_in_the_United_States" title="Community colleges in the United States">community colleges</a> and technical schools that are separate from the PASSHE system. Additionally there are many private two- and four-year <a href="/wiki/Technical_school" title="Technical school">technical schools</a>, colleges and universities.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University" title="Carnegie Mellon University">Carnegie Mellon University</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Pennsylvania_State_University" title="The Pennsylvania State University">The Pennsylvania State University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a>, are members of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_American_Universities" title="Association of American Universities">Association of American Universities</a>, an invitation only organization of leading research universities. <a href="/wiki/Lehigh_University" title="Lehigh University">Lehigh University</a> is also another renowned private research university located in Bethlehem, Pennsylvania.The Pennsylvania State University is the Commonwealth's <a href="/wiki/Land-grant_university" title="Land-grant university">Land-grant university</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Sea_Grant_College_Program" title="National Sea Grant College Program">Sea Grant College</a> and, <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Grant_College_and_Fellowship_Program" title="National Space Grant College and Fellowship Program">Space Grant College</a>. The University of Pennsylvania, located in Philadelphia, is considered the <a href="/wiki/First_university_in_the_United_States" title="First university in the United States">first university in the United States</a> and established the country's <a href="/wiki/First_university_in_the_United_States#Establishment_of_quartary-education_schools.2C_issuance_of_any_kind_of_.22doctoral.22_degree" title="First university in the United States">first</a> <a href="/wiki/Medical_school_in_the_United_States" title="Medical school in the United States">medical school</a>. The University of Pennsylvania is also the Commonwealth's only, and geographically the most southern, <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> school. The <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Academy_of_the_Fine_Arts" title="Pennsylvania Academy of the Fine Arts">Pennsylvania Academy of the Fine Arts</a> is the first and oldest <a href="/wiki/Art_school" title="Art school">art school</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pafa.org_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-pafa.org-148">[148]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_College_of_Pharmacy" title="Philadelphia College of Pharmacy">Philadelphia College of Pharmacy</a>, now a part of <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Sciences" title="University of the Sciences">University of the Sciences in Philadelphia</a>, was the first <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy_school" title="Pharmacy school">pharmacy school</a> in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usciences.edu_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-usciences.edu-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pennsylvania&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="673" data-file-width="1073" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg/220px-Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg/330px-Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg/440px-Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dorney_Park_Steel_Force_Thunderhawk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dorney_Park_%26_Wildwater_Kingdom" title="Dorney Park &amp; Wildwater Kingdom">Dorney Park and Wildwater Kingdom's</a> <a href="/wiki/Steel_Force" title="Steel Force">Steel Force</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thunderhawk_(Dorney_Park)" title="Thunderhawk (Dorney Park)">Thunderhawk</a> roller coasters in <a href="/wiki/Allentown,_Pennsylvania" title="Allentown, Pennsylvania">Allentown</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pennsylvania is home to the nation's first zoo, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Zoo" title="Philadelphia Zoo">Philadelphia Zoo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Other long-accredited AZA zoos include the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Zoo" title="Erie Zoo">Erie Zoo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Zoo_%26_PPG_Aquarium" title="Pittsburgh Zoo &amp; PPG Aquarium">Pittsburgh Zoo &amp; PPG Aquarium</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Lehigh_Valley_Zoo" title="Lehigh Valley Zoo">Lehigh Valley Zoo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hersheypark" title="Hersheypark">ZOOAMERICA</a> are other notable zoos. The Commonwealth boasts some of the finest museums in the country, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnegie_Museums" title="Carnegie Museums">Carnegie Museums</a> in Pittsburgh, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Museum_of_Art" title="Philadelphia Museum of Art">Philadelphia Museum of Art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Category:Museums_in_Pennsylvania" title="Category:Museums in Pennsylvania">several others</a>. One unique museum is the <a href="/wiki/Houdini_Museum" title="Houdini Museum">Houdini Museum</a> in Scranton, the only building in the world devoted to the legendary magician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Pennsylvania is also home to the <a href="/wiki/National_Aviary" title="National Aviary">National Aviary</a>, located in Pittsburgh.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:640px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Baughman%27s_Rock_Overlook.ogv/640px--Baughman%27s_Rock_Overlook.ogv.jpg&quot;
